                         A table of Pseudorandom numbers
                   draft-robinson-pseudorandom-numbers-00.txt

     Abstract
          This document provides a set of several thousand standardized
          hexadecimal pseudorandom values for use in creating repeatable
          sequences of so-called "random" numbers for various purposes.  It
          can also provide a set of seed values for encryption or
          compression programs or any other use of a large set of numbers. 
          It also provides a reference listing for the MD5 and CRC values of
          all prior published on-line Internet RFCs.  

          Distribution of this memo is unlimited and unrestricted. 
          Reproduction of all or part of this document for any purpose is
          permitted, and without any requirement to secure permission or
          payment of royalty.

     1.   Introduction

          There have been various tables of numbers to be used for "seeding"
          random number generators for testing and other purposes.  Most of
          which do not have a large enough base of numbers available for
          very long sequences of random numbers available and can cause
          repetition over a short span.  This document proposes to provide a
          set of numbers which may be used for providing a consistent table. 
          This also contains the MD5 checksum as well as the CRC checksum
          values for all RFCs published prior to the creation of this
          document.  This also provides a set of fixed and published numeric
          values which may be used for any other useful purpose including
          one-time keys for encryption or authentication or any other
          purpose where a large number (in this case, over 8000) of stable
          numeric values are desired.

          In essence, this provides several thousand 32-bit and 256-bit
          published, reproducible binary numbers (in the sense that anyone
          else running MD5 or CRC-32 should get the same results) and which
          may be used for any purpose without requirement of permission or
          payment of royalty.

          The author encourages suggestions on other checksum processes
          which may be used for creation of additional numeric values for
          use in this document, and for reporting of any possible errors
          which might have occurred.


     2.   Method of creation of this table

          The standard archive file of all text-based RFCs was obtained from
          the [RFC-Editor website].  All of the individual files were
          extracted and passed through a program producing the MD5 algorithm
          as given in [RFC1321] to produce the MD5 checksum value, and by a
          standard Zip un-archiver to obtain the CRC-32 value, for each
          file.  The actual size of the file in bytes as is stated in the
          zip archive was used.  No alteration of the text of the RFCs was
          made, in order to ensure that the number sequences provided herein
          can be repeated by others.

          The archives in question were downloaded on "Sat April 15 17:13:00 
          2006 EDT" (-0400 UTC) from the URLs 
          ftp://ftp.rfc-editor.org/in-notes/tar/RFC-all.zip, and
          ftp://ftp.rfc-editor.org/in-notes/tar/RFC-all.tar.gz.  A note in
          the file "nfs/ftp/in-notes/tar/readme.txt" inside both the .zip
          and .tar files stated that the archive was created on "Sat Apr 15
          12:30:00 PDT 2006" (-0700 UTC).  The subsidiary RFC documents from
          each archive were each extracted into separate directories in the
          event the .tar archive files were different from the .zip archive
          files (which, as it turns out, they were).  Additionally, the tar
          archive file was separately extracted from the .tar.gz container





          INTERNET DRAFT           April 15, 2006                  Robinson
             draft-robinson-pseudorandom-numbers-00.txt       Page 4 of 181

          file before further processing.  The primary three files were also
          processed through a zip program without further compression to
          obtain the CRC-32 value of, and through an MD5 generator program
          to obtain as is the MD5 signature of these bare archive files as
          downloaded.  

          The results of these actions are as follows:

          File Name     Size      CRC-32    MD5
          .zip          77210183  f3cc1659  e99aefa3052539a95a22267390f3e607
          rfc-all.tar  260310016  6920a222  90e71a518e985f7c680ba251aed94f0e
          .tar.gz       73903752  3d2a7196  502ad8fc823b5c57bef981f73ff3bf16

          A similar table (minus the MD5 listing, which is too wide), can be
          shown here for comparison:

          File Name    zip file         rfc-all.tar        .tar.gz file 
          File Size    77,210,183        260,310,016        73,903,752   
          CRC-32       f3cc1659          6920a222           3d2a7196
          Enclosed files    4,464              4,466                 1

          The two files that appear in the .tar.gz archive that do not
          appear in the zip archive are "rfc1279.log" and "rfc1305.tar".

          Highest numbered RFC in archive is 4472

          In examining all the files in each archive, it appears (as would
          be correct practice) that the member files in the ZIP archive are
          all slightly larger than the tar member files, the tar members
          being terminated by LF and the zip members being terminated by
          CRLF.  This would make sense, as typically files in a ZIP archive
          would usually be processed on machines which use CRLF as a line
          terminator, and files in a TAR archive would usually be processed
          on machines which use LF as a line terminator.  For that reason,
          the result numbers for both groups of files will be included.

     3.   Explanation of meaning of this table.

          The lines of entries consist of two lines for each RFC.  There is
          a header above all the lines summarizing column values.   

          The first column is the RFC number, which has been zero-filled for
          consistency.  The second column is the file type, which is
          explained below.   The third column is the decimal size in 8-bit
          bytes of the file of that type.  The fourth column is the 8-byte
          hexadecimal CRC-32 value.  The fifth column is the 32-byte
          hexadecimal MD5 256-bit value.

          Where the file type is indicated 
          - ZIP it is the .txt file in the .zip archive; 
          - TAR it is the .txt file in the .tar archive;





          INTERNET DRAFT           April 15, 2006                  Robinson
             draft-robinson-pseudorandom-numbers-00.txt       Page 5 of 181

          - PS  it is a Postscript file and 
          - PDF it is a PDF document 
           (which both ps and PDF are always the same for both archives). 

          To assist in locating values through the table of contents, a
          marker is included at each thousands boundary.
